10.1016/j.jmp.2021.102534
In-play betting markets allow bettors to bet or liquidate (i.e.sell) bets mid-game. Products allowing the bettor to fully or partially liquidate (or ‘hedge out of’) existing bets, known as ‘early cash-out’, are popular with bookmakers and bettors alike ( Lopez-Gonzalez & Griffiths, 2017 ). The bettor can exchange an uncertain future payoff for a guaranteed amount. If odds have moved in the bettor’s favour, the bettor can take out an immediate gain. For example, if the bettor made a $1 bet that would repay $3 in the event of the bettor winning, and during the game that outcome becomes more probable, the betting agency will offer to reverse the bet, or any fraction of it, if the bettor accepts a payout of (say) $2.50 per $1 bet. Alternatively, if the odds have moved unfavourably, the bettor can ‘cut their losses’ and take some cash from a stake that is now looking less likely to win and may prove worthless. For example, the bettor who made a $1 bet might accept a payment of $0.30 to get out rather than risk losing her full $1 bet.

For the bookmaker, offering early cash-outs at less than their expected value can be not only profitable on average but can also help to reduce cash-flow volatility (ups and downs) while potentially attracting customers and generating greater trade. 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         In stock broking, there is an expression “churn ‘em and burn ‘em”, meaning that the more that clients are encouraged to buy and sell, the greater the broker’s commissions, regardless of whether the client wins or loses by the extra trading. 
                       Bettors generally like the flexibility of cash-outs. The possibility of cashing in and out of bets makes the betting ‘game’ potentially more entertaining and even more of a strategic contest between their probability assessments and the bookmaker’s posted prices.

Newall (2017)  notes that bookmakers are likely to make cash-out offers so as to capitalise on the behavioural biases of bettors, who simply sell apparently winning bets below their expected value, but do not sell their losing positions. This bias has an analogy in conventional financial markets, in which individual investors subject to the ‘disposition effect’ (e.g.,  Odean, 1998; Shefrin & Statman, 1985 ) tend to sell winning stocks, but hold onto losers. Note however that those authors considered the S-shaped utility function in prospect theory but did not allow for probability weighting. As betting markets include bets with odds-implied probabilities across the spectrum, accounting for probability weighting is important.

Our paper explores whether a bettor will decide to cash-out early, and if so, in what amount and under what circumstances. We assume that the bettor’s motivation is described by cumulative prospect theory (henceforth CPT), ( Tversky & Kahneman, 1992 ). Evidence appears to support the common characterisation of gamblers as subject to all the ‘biases’ assumed of decision makers under CPT, including probability weighting (e.g. see most recently  Andrikogiannopoulou & Papakonstantinou, 2020; Chiappori, Salanié, Salanié, & Gandhi, 2019 ).

Under CPT, the bettor (i) is risk-averse over gains and risk-seeking and loss-averse over losses, and (ii) exhibits inverse S-shaped probability weighting functions, which means that they overweight small probabilities. Specifically, when the probability of winning is very high they overstate the small probability of losing, and when the probability of winning is very low they overstate that small probability. We find the optimal amount for a bettor, who is subject to CPT preferences and biases, to cash-out, allowing for the domain of possible initial and updated (in-play) probabilities of winning.

Earlier research by  Kyle, Ou-Yang, and Xiong (2006)  used unweighted prospect theory to explain how investors holding a risky stake face an “optimal stopping problem” in which the solution under loss aversion and unweighted prospect theory is commonly to realise their investment before its natural termination. We extend the prospect theory explanation of early termination by allowing for any degree of partial termination and also for the extra effects of the probability weighting function within CPT. 
                         2 
                      
                      
                         2 
                         
                            Lopez-Gonzalez, Guerrero-Solé, Estevez, and Griffiths (2018)  draw an analogy between gambling markets and the stock market, as typically used by advertisers, cashing-out is equivalent to ‘selling down stock’. We do not address issues of probability formation in this paper, nor provide an explanation of the preferences that lead the bettor to place their initial stake. However,  Hing, Li, Vitartas, and Russell (2018)  argue that in-play bettors are more likely to bet on impulse, suggesting that the cognitive processes involved are quite different from those that are likely to be used in pre-match betting.

Before presenting our more general theory, we illustrate the gambler’s problem and potential CPT behaviour with a numerical example where the gambler can choose to cash-out a thus-far winning bet, or thus-far losing bet. The bettor has placed an initial $1 stake on an outcome with a (gross) win payoff of

Hence, as in  Peel and Law (2009) , the dollar gain or loss on the bet is also the bettor’s CPT psychological gain or loss, because the natural psychological reference point is starting wealth. As in  Kyle et al. (2006) , we assume “narrow framing” ( Barberis, Huang, & Thaler, 2006 ) under which any contemporaneous bets or investments do not affect the bettor’s psychological treatment of the bet in question.

A representative example of the CPT value function is shown in  Fig. 1 . The S-shaped value function reflects ‘diminishing sensitivity’ in both the gain and loss domains (i.e. decreasing marginal utility/disutility of gains and losses) and reflects risk-aversion in the domain of gains and risk-seeking in the domain of losses. The steeper slope on the loss part (left of the y-axis) reflects the degree of loss aversion of the bettor (here, the coefficient of loss aversion,

                               = 
                               2 
                            
                         , so the loss of $1 feels twice as bad as a gain of $1 feels good).

This simple example of itself shows why bettors appreciate having the option to cash-out their bets. Without that option, an action that is psychologically very attractive is unavailable, and the bettor must sit out the whole game on tenterhooks worried about the potential loss of the bet even when losing has much lower probability than when the bet was made.

Probability weighting in the CPT model takes the form of inverse-S-shaped weighting functions, with separate functions for gains and losses. The intuition is quite simple overall. When the bettor’s objective probability of winning is high, she biases it downwards in her mind, which, when combined with the small marginal utility of a marginally bigger gain, biases her decision towards cashing-out. That bias is greatly exacerbated by her upwardly biased probability of losing and the relatively very large disutility of losses under loss aversion. These four psychological forces are all pushing her towards cashing-out.

Suppose as above that a bettor has placed an initial $1 stake with the prospect of a  
                            ω

As the CPT bettor is risk-seeking in the domain of losses, even if offered the fair price of $0.50 to cash-out their bet, the bettor would likely prefer to hold their initial position. The intuition is that a $1 loss is only marginally worse in utility than a $0.525 loss, but the marginal utility of any gain or partial recovery is large. An intermediate position may, in fact, be preferable. If the bettor can partially sell out of their initial bet, they can take back some cash, and possibly crystallise some profit, while retaining potentially more profit from the holding component. This comes from the ‘kink’ observed at the status quo (break-even point) for the bettor under CPT, 
                            3 
                         
                         
                            3 
                            That kink is what leads to possible violation under CPT of the ‘betweenness axiom’ (e.g.  Camerer & Ho, 1994; Prelec, 1990 ). Note that the betweenness axiom is not relevant in our model because partial cashing-out is not a probability mixture of holding and fully cashing-out. 
                          which as the effect that in general the preclusion of a loss of $ x  has a much greater psychological attraction than adding $x of possible profit.

Partially cashing-out is potentially valuable also for the bettor sitting on a loss. Here, the bettor may prefer to cash-out just enough of the initial bet to retain the possibility of breaking even while avoiding a total loss.

revealing that the option to partially cash-out has highest expected utility and presents the bettor with an acceptable hedge relative to either fully cashing-out (and accepting a large loss) or holding (and risking a total loss).

Note that the option to partially cash-out expands the bettor’s opportunity set, especially if the bookmaker allows any fraction  
                            
                               c 
                               < 
                               1 
                            
                         , which for an economically rational gambler is valuable, and for a CPT gambler is potentially very attractive psychologically.

The illustrative examples in Section  2  reveal how the bettor’s decision to cash-out, or to partially cash-out, depends on the parameters of their CPT utility function. We now formalise the problem so as to reach more general results.

Suppose (without loss of generality) that the bettor holds a $1 stake on a game outcome

                               1

                            κ

Lemmas 1 and 2  work together as follows. If  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     − 
                                  
                               
                               < 
                               1 
                            
                         , the bookmaker offers the bettor more than their initial stake to cash-out their bet, and so cashing-out  
                            
                               c 
                               = 
                               1 
                            
                          would lead to a certain gain. In this scenario, the bettor is in the domain of gains. In the other case,  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     + 
                                  
                               
                               < 
                               1 
                            
                         , the bettor is in the domain of losses, as exercising the option to cash-out would yield a certain loss. From this, we obtain thresholds on the cash-out proportions. In case (a), cashing-out an amount  
                            
                               c 
                               > 
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     +

                               y 
                               = 
                               1

                               c 
                               > 
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     − 
                                  
                               
                            
                          will yield a gain for the bettor even if the initial bet is unsuccessful ( 
                            
                               y 
                               = 
                               0 
                            
                         ).

For future reference we will also compute the derivatives of the utility function. As the utility function is piecewise differentiable, we search for the highest utility level along the different ‘pieces’ of the utility function. Recall that the function to be optimised,  
                            
                               u 
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                               
                            
                         , is piecewise differentiable. The optimisation problem is thus reduced to finding the optimal solution along each piece, and then comparing these local maxima. For example, the utility of cashing-out a fraction at the interior point  
                            
                               
                                  c 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         , found at the local maximum in the segment

                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               ≤ 
                               1 
                            
                         , needs to be compared with the utility of not cashing-out ( 
                            
                               c 
                               = 
                               0 
                            
                         ) and the utility of cashing-out completely  
                            
                               
                                  c 
                                  = 
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         .

In the specific cases from  Theorem 1 , labelled (a) through (d), specific parameter values determine the optimal fraction for the bettor to cash-out. Thus, the deciding factor as to whether the bettor would prefer to cash-out partially or completely is the loss aversion coefficient.

To shed further light on the model of cashing-out under CPT, in this section we explore how parameter values affect the bettor’s choice. 
                            4 
                         
                         
                            4 
                            There is significant existing literature in CPT (e.g.  Broomell & Bhatia, 2014; Nilsson, Rieskamp, & Wagenmakers, 2011 ) concerning the estimation of realistic CPT parameter values and the sensitivity of individuals’ decisions to ‘their’ personal parameters.

In  Fig. 7  we present the optimal cash-out fraction,

The sub-figures in  Fig. 7  are divided into regions, cut off by borders denoted ‘A’, and ‘B’, (with the addition of ‘C’ in the case of the left figure). The boundaries represent, respectively: the region for which cashing-out is not optimal in the loss domain (A), the peak separating the loss domain from the gain domain (B), and the ‘ridge’ for which full cash-out is optimal in the domain of gains (C in the left figure).

From  Fig. 7 , we can observe the difference that the probability weighting and loss aversion parameters make to the bettor’s decision. The flat region cut off by the curve A, where not cashing-out is optimal in the domain of losses, extends further towards​  
                            
                               ω 
                               = 
                               1 
                            
                          in the left-figure. This reflects the impact of the behavioural tendencies; bettors exhibiting probability weighting and loss aversion are more likely to liquidate bets on favourites ( 
                            
                               ω 
                               < 
                               2 
                               
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          at a loss compared with bettors without these biases. The presence of the ‘ridge’ at which full cash-out is optimal is clear from the left figure. In the domain of gains, without probability weighting or loss aversion, the proportion of winning bets cashed-out actually decreases with the likelihood of winning (i.e. as the current odds approach 1). However, for the bettor with probability weighting and loss aversion, the ‘ridge’ is at the level of  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               1 
                            
                         . There is also a noticeable minimum value for the partial cash-out in the domain of gains in the left-hand figure, consistent with the minimum value in  Fig. 4 .

It is this ‘ridge’ value, where the bettor is in the domain of gains and the bettor increases their tendency to cash-out, that appears to be mainly driven by behavioural biases. This region stems from probability weighting, as the bettor can cash-out a sufficient amount to avoid the loss domain entirely, reducing the impact of the loss aversion coefficient. It is this region that contains the likely winning bets that bookmakers would prefer bettors to liquidate.

Next, we examine the impact of the loss aversion parameter,  
                            λ 
                         , without changing the level of probability weighting. In  Fig. 8 , we present the optimal cash-out fraction  
                            
                               
                                  c 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         , by the level of current odds and initial odds,  
                            ω

                            λ

                               ν

Fig. 8  shows that cashing-out in the domain of losses (for fixed levels of probability weighting) is highly sensitive to the level of loss aversion. Indeed, the region cut off by the sector A decreases with the level of loss aversion; the bettor would almost entirely avoid cashing-out in the domain of losses in the rightmost subfigure (where  
                            
                               λ 
                               = 
                               1 
                               . 
                               75 
                               
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         .

Effectively, in the domain of losses, probability weighting drives the bettor to hold onto unlikely winning bets. With increased levels of loss aversion, the prospect of losing the entire initial stake begins to loom larger, and the bettor will liquidate part of their bet to avoid this ‘worst-case scenario’ in all but the most unlikely of wins. From  Fig. 8 , we can also see that the loss aversion parameter does not appear to affect the decision to cash-out in the domain of gains. This is because the bettor can partially cash-out sufficiently to avoid the possibility of a loss, rendering the loss aversion parameter irrelevant.

In the domain of gains, particularly when the likelihood of winning is high, probability weighting and loss aversion tend to heavily increase the proportion of the bet cashed-out. Consider the ‘Moderate Favourite’ example. An initial wager at odds of $1.50, which implies an initial probability of 1/1.50

In case (i), the bettor would not choose to cash-out, but with the addition of either loss aversion (as in case (ii)), probability weighting (as in case (iii)), or both (case (iv)), the bettor would prefer to cash-out a substantial portion of their bet (or entirely as in the latter two cases).

In the domain of losses (Panel B), loss aversion is the prominent driver of the decision to cash-out at all (as seen in  Fig. 8 ), with probability weighting playing a lesser role. As an example, consider the case of ‘Moderate Underdog.’ The bettor placed a stake at initial odds of  
                            
                               γ 
                               = 
                               $ 
                               3 
                               . 
                               00

                               κ 
                               = 
                               $ 
                               0 
                               . 
                               713 
                            
                         . The optimal cash-out fraction in case (i) is  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗

                               0 
                               .

The final analysis we consider is how the liquidation cost,  
                            γ

Logically, we should expect that the bettor will prefer to reduce their fraction cashed-out if the cost of liquidation increases. Indeed, this appears to be the case. The evidence presented in  Fig. 9  shows that the region bounded by A, where the bettor chooses not to cash-out, increases with the liquidation cost. There is close to a ‘step’ function; provided the bettor exhibits sufficient loss aversion they will still be willing to cash-out even in the presence of a large liquidation cost. There is only a small drop-off in the fraction the bettor is willing to cash-out (conditional on wanting to do so) as the transaction cost rises. The ‘ridge’ region cut off by the line B, beyond which the bettor prefers to completely cash-out, is substantially reduced as  
                            γ 
                          increases. The proportion of the bettor’s winnings foregone is too great to warrant a cash-out in all but the most extreme probabilities of winning.

Overall, this suggests that a reduction in the propensity to cash-out could be achieved by increasing the cost of doing so. If this were set by the bookmaker, for example, then the bookmaker would face fewer, but more profitable (on average) opportunities to extract profits from the bettor. Discussion

Full or partial cashing-out of bets is equivalent to investors in the stock market selling all or part of their stock holdings. 
                            5 
                         
                         
                            5 
                            Prior empirical research has identified that bettors prefer to sell winning, rather than losing bets (e.g.  Hartzmark & Solomon, 2012 ), consistent with disposition effect exhibited by individual investors in financial markets (e.g.  Odean, 1998 ).  Brown and Yang (2017)  examined a change to a betting exchange in which the cash-out feature was made more salient. Bettors could already (effectively) replicate a cash-out by laying off their initial bet, but when prominently presented as a ‘cash-out’, the empirical disposition effect was increased. 
                          Further analogies are nearly as obvious. For example, a real estate investor who buys units in an investment trust is in a position at any time to sell any number of those units. There is extensive empirical research suggesting that stock market investors are prone to sell winners and ‘ride losers’, a disposition that has been explained in behavioural​ finance by  Odean (1998)  and others as a product of the S-shaped prospect theory utility or value function.

Our CPT analysis extends what has been shown empirically in stock market investment by exploiting the simplicity of bets relative to stocks and other investments. In betting the possible outcome is binary rather than continuous, and there is a distinct end date at which the “investment” is clearly successful or not. Although different in some ways, 
                            6 
                         
                         
                            6 
                            Betting markets are inherently negative sum games whereas stock markets are historically positive sum games in which it is possible for even naïve investors to profit (e.g., anyone who bought a random selection of stocks 20 years ago is virtually guaranteed now to be vastly better off in the value of those stocks). In other ways, picking stocks is no different to picking winners in horse racing or sports betting. 
                          bets are in essence investments with an inherent simplicity that allows formal mathematical characterisation of circumstances under which full, part or nil cash-out is ‘optimal’ to a characteristically CPT bettor.

We have derived the optimal cash-out fractions for a CPT bettor who has placed a unit $1 stake and is offered a once-only opportunity to liquidate their bet (fully or partially), for a fee, during the running of that event (i.e. ‘in-play’). We find a case-based, analytic solution that allows for the discontinuous piecewise nature of the CPT utility function. Our solution is general, with the only restriction placed on the CPT model of  Tversky and Kahneman (1992)  being that the utility curvature parameter is equal in the domains of gains and losses (i.e.  
                            
                               α 
                               = 
                               β 
                               
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         .

Our results shed light into the psychological drivers of early liquidation for bettors who realistically exhibit CPT preferences and inherent biases. It is increasingly accepted that gamblers’ behaviours​ are consistent with CPT ( Andrikogiannopoulou & Papakonstantinou, 2020; Chiappori et al., 2019 ).

In the domain of gains, where the bookmaker offers the bettor an immediate guaranteed profit to liquidate their stake, the bettor can effectively eliminate the possibility of a loss by cashing-out enough of their bet that their profit is non-negative ( 
                            
                               
                                  
                                     g 
                                  
                                  
                                     − 
                                  
                               
                               ≥ 
                               0 
                            
                          in our technical parlance). While a prospect theory bettor not subject to probability weighting biases might prefer to cash-out partially in the domain of gains, we demonstrate that probability weighting both increases their tendency to liquidate, and increases the fraction cashed-out when cashing-out is optimal. Specifically, we document that fully cashing-out (based on the condition  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               > 
                               1 
                            
                         ) is only optimal as the impact of the distorted probability  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     w 
                                  
                                  
                                     + 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     p 
                                  
                               
                            
                          is overstated relative to the liquidation cost,  
                            γ 
                         .

From our numerical example in  Table 3 , the bettor who exhibits loss aversion, but not probability weighting, would prefer to cash-out  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     c 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               71 
                            
                         .9% of their bet, as opposed to the complete liquidation preferred by the bettor who exhibits probability weighting  and  loss aversion. The differences between these two fractions are representative of the additional liquidation motivated by probability weighting. This is costly on average for the bettor, who sells more winning bets. The foregone profits and liquidation costs brought by selling winning bets lead to lower average profits, while the general reluctance to sell losing bets does not cauterise losses. In other words, bettors with behavioural biases who use cash-outs, on average, sell the upside, but not the downside on their bets, as is surely anticipated by the bookmakers who offer cash-outs.

The contention that bookmakers offer the cash-out products to extract additional revenue from their clients appears to be thoroughly well-founded, presuming that the average bettor exhibits CPT-style preferences. A bettor who exaggerates the probability of their winning bet becoming an eventual loser is more likely to cash-out than one who does not. From the perspective of the bettor, the decision to cash-out would look wise in hindsight if the initial bet does end up losing. However, since bookmakers are generally very well-calibrated forecasters ( Forrest, Goddard, & Simmons, 2005; Grant & Johnstone, 2010 ), this is not likely to be a reliable growth strategy for the bettor over the long term, especially when early liquidation has a material fee or transaction cost.

Hing et al. (2018)  and  LaPlante, Nelson, and Gray (2014)  (among others) argue that problem gamblers are more likely to engage with in-play betting products, including cash-out products. Moreover,  Lopez-Gonzalez and Griffiths (2017)  suggest that the cash-out option transforms sports betting into a continuous (rather than discrete) activity. Beyond this,  Killick and Griffiths (2019) ,  inter alia , note that in-play sports betting may be more hazardous to problem gamblers because it requires that the bettor is willing to make rapid or impulsive decisions without time for reflection or good assessment.

Gainsbury, Liu, Russell and Teichert (2016)  suggest that the availability of gambling on mobile devices may exacerbate temptation and losses for problem gamblers.  Lopez-Gonzalez and Griffiths (2017)  argue that the use of smartphones increases the bettor’s “illusion of control”, in which the bettor’s expectation of success is higher than warranted.  Killick and Griffiths (2019)  note that in-play betting has the potential to magnify the illusion of control because bettors are able to dictate the timing, frequency and size of wagers, enhancing their perception of control over trades and outcomes.

Similarly,  Lopez-Gonzalez, Stavros, and Smith (2019)  explain that the use of mobile devices as ‘second screens’ when viewing sports has appeared to increase engagement with bookmakers. To compound this, cash-out products are in many countries advertised frequently in the media (including saliently during televised sporting events), promoting the convenience, sophistication and ‘fun’ of using smartphones to bet in-play. One advertising campaign frames the cash-out product in terms of avoiding the ‘curse’ placed on the bettor’s team by their friend’s over-stated mid-game certainty (‘they cannot lose from here’). The implicit point is that ‘you are smarter than your friend’ and you can trade on that savvy and expertise.

James, O’Malley, and Tunney (2017)  cautions that problem gamblers are likely to engage with multiple forms of gambling, including online, smartphone, and in-play. The hypothesis is that in-play continuous betting opportunities, and a shorter delay between wager, outcome, and reward, can drive more gambling and more risky gambling.

Odean (1998)  used actual client trading data to reveal the money and opportunity losses incurred by stock market traders when riding losers and prematurely selling gains. Prior research in stock markets has argued that advances in investor sophistication (e.g.  Feng & Seasholes, 2005 ) and greater trading experience (e.g.  Seru, Shumway, & Stoffman, 2010 ) can help reduce the impact of the disposition effect. Traders’ outcomes improve, in other words, with greater self-awareness, self-control and ‘learning’ to realise losses early. The same potential remedies apply to ‘problem gamblers’.

In betting markets, bettors appear to cash-out winning bets as compared to losing bets in approximately a 3:1 ratio (e.g.  Brown & Yang, 2017 ), a similar ratio to that observed in the stock market (e.g.  Odean, 1998 ). Based on the theoretical CPT framework uncovered in this paper, we argue that probability weighting appears to be the main driver of early cash-out behaviour in betting markets. While both loss aversion and probability weighting drive the bettor to prefer to cash-out, it is the bettors tendency to understate the probability of winning when it is high that clinches the choice to sell out of the very bets that are best held. These have a high probability of winning yet the bookmaker manages to buy them back advantageously at prices as if that probability is materially lower.

Reducing the impact of probability weighting is an issue that has been explored, but rarely within the gambling context. Research in psychology, indicates that presentation formats, including the use of graphs and non-numerical messages, can reduce the degree of bias in probability estimates (e.g.  Visschers, Meertens, Passchier, & de Vries, 2009 ).  Sun, Zhang, Sai, and Hu (2018)  argue that self-distancing – or taking a third-person perspective in making a decision – reduces the level of probability weighting exhibited by experimental participants, while self-immersion increases an individual’s level of probability distortion. Related work by  Lermer, Streicher, Sachs, Raue, and Frey (2016)  argues that concrete thinking will increase the perceived risk level in probability judgements, while abstract thinking reduces perceived risks.  Polman (2012)  demonstrates in several experimental tasks (including a gambling task) that decisions made on behalf of others exhibit lower levels of loss aversion than decisions made for oneself. This supports the contention that psychological distance, be it in the form of abstraction or delegation, can help to reduce the impact of behavioural biases in risky choices.

Betting markets represent a difficult setting for implementing de-biasing techniques. Gamblers, and particularly problem gamblers, appear highly prone to engage with advertising and social media (e.g.  Gainsbury, King et al., 2016 ). Those favouring in-play bets, such as cash-outs, appear more susceptible to gambling on impulse ( Hing et al., 2018 ). For this subset of individuals, the psychological approaches to debiasing probabilities may be difficult to implement (realistically).

One potential solution clearly not favoured by bookmakers is to give bettors a mechanism to preset ‘stop-loss’ orders, which automatically liquidate bets at pre-committed levels ( Fischbacher, Hoffmann, & Schudy, 2017 ). Stop-loss orders, offered by most stockbrokers, allow traders to lock themselves into exiting their bets when a loss is imminent, before that loss becomes larger and possibly very damaging. For example, if the bettor’s initial $1 stake reduces in realisable value to $0.80, the bet can be automatically liquidated, and by making such a precommitment the bettor protects herself from what she knows she might do – i.e. to ride the loss and hope that it recovers.

Shefrin and Statman (1985)  propose the use of stop-loss orders in reducing the disposition effect in stock markets, in an effort to circumvent an investor’s self-control deficiencies. In the financial market setting, there is some evidence that the effective use of stop loss orders can lower the impact of the disposition effect (e.g.  Richards, Rutterford, Kodwani, & Fenton-O’Creevy, 2017; Talpsepp & Vaarmets, 2019 ) for retail investors. There remains however some debate as to whether in stock markets the use of stop-loss orders is preferable on average to simply buying and holding (e.g.  Kaminski & Lo, 2014; Lo & Remorov, 2017 ). In our model, the bookmaker offers cash-outs specifically to discourage the investor from buying and holding, and wins on average over all cash-out transactions by paying the bettor less than the expected payoff of every bet bought back. It is fair to say therefore that on average, or over the long run, bettors would benefit by never cashing-out, however bettors cannot adopt the long run average strategy that a bookmaker can. For one thing, a small capital endowment and a run of bad luck can ruin a bettor, or a bettor’s life, in just a very short run.

Working on the basis that what is good for the bookmaker is bad for the bettor, a pre-commitment by bettors whose bets are relatively small (i.e. not large enough to ruin them) to never use cash-outs may be more effective for those bettors than use of a stop-loss mechanism, especially given that stop-losses do not protect the bettor from selling out of winners before the big wins accrue. Whether bettors are better to hold or cash-out on average is an empirical question. Welfare agencies and regulators will see little advantage either way, since any extra profits be not cashing out, or any reduction in losses by cashing out, have the one effect of encouraging problem gamblers to keep gambling.

A major difficulty faced by bettors when considering the use of ‘cash-out’ products is the lack of transparency over the transaction cost of cashing-out. In this paper, we have modelled the cash-out cost,  
                         γ 
                      , as constant independent of  
                         p 
                      . Bookmakers do not typically advertise, nor even post, their pricing schedule for early liquidation. Anecdotally, there is some indication that bookmakers do not charge liquidation costs equally across the possible range of odds  
                         
                            p 
                            ∕ 
                            
                               ( 
                               1 
                               − 
                               p 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      ; rather they tend to charge lower commissions for cashing-out likely winners compared with likely losers. That strategic price setting by bookmakers is consistent with the  Levitt (2004)  model in which bookmakers’ prices are set so as to best exploit bettors’ psychological biases and deficiencies.

The worldwide introduction by sophisticated on-line bookmakers of cash-out facilities can be understood as another such strategic initiative, making it all the more important that psychology reaches a rigorous understanding of how bettors are likely to respond and be affected by that extra opportunity to make or more generally lose money.
